Source: 2026-05-31 How Google BigQuery Works: Architecture, Components, and Query Execution | by Nikhil Mogre | MediumType: Pasted TextWebSync metadata title: How Google BigQuery Works: Architecture, Components, and Query Execution | by Nikhil Mogre | Medium url: https://medium.com/@nikhilmogre1998/how-google-bigquery-works-architecture-components-and-query-execution-db8b24c23946 date: 2026-05-31T01:29:14.389Z parsing method: defuddle Sitemap BigQuery Architecture B igQuery is Google’s fully managed, serverless cloud data warehouse—a system built to run SQL analytics on petabytes of data without requiring any cluster setup or tuning. In practice, you load data and issue SQL queries. BigQuery automatically provisions thousands of CPUs on Google’s infrastructure and returns results in seconds. It scales transparently: there are no servers for you to manage, and you pay only for the storage and compute (slots) you actually use. This massively parallel, on-demand execution model means you get “warehouse” performance without the overhead of traditional data warehouse clusters. What we are going to cover in this blog: How BigQuery’s serverless and fully managed model works Understanding BigQuery’s decoupled architecture (separate compute and storage) Core internal components of BigQuery (Capacitor, Colossus, Dremel, Borg, Jupiter) How a BigQuery SQL query is executed step by step Explanation of Dremel’s tree architecture (Root node, Intermediate (mixer) nodes, Leaf nodes) What slots are in BigQuery, and how they affect performance and cost Why BigQuery scales automatically and handles massive datasets efficiently Decoupled Storage and Compute A key innovation in BigQuery is the separation of storage and compute. Storage lives in Colossus (Google’s distributed file system) and compute runs in Dremel query clusters managed by Borg, all connected by Google’s high-speed Jupiter network. In other words, BigQuery stores your data and executes your queries on completely independent layers. This decoupling is deliberate: BigQuery “separates the concepts of storage (Colossus) and compute (Borg) and allows them to scale independently”. You can grow your data volume without provisioning extra CPUs, and run large queries by spinning up compute without changing storage. BigQuery’s architecture separates storage (left, Colossus) from compute (right, Dremel via Borg). The fast Jupiter network links them, enabling high-throughput data shuffling. This design delivers huge flexibility and efficiency. Because storage and compute scale on demand, you never pay for idle infrastructure. You pay only for the petabytes of data stored in Colossus and the slot-seconds (compute) used by your queries. By contrast, traditional data warehouses force you to buy fixed clusters (often wasting resources). BigQuery’s model is cost-efficient and elastic: Google’s Borg scheduler can instantly spin up thousands of parallel workers for big queries, then tear them down when done. In short, BigQuery gives you enterprise-scale performance without managing hardware. Core Components of BigQuery Dremel Engine Working how it performs MPP massive parallel processing BigQuery is built on five key Google technologies, each handling one aspect of the system. Together, they enable high performance, scalability, and reliability: Capacitor (Columnar Storage Format) — BigQuery stores table data in a proprietary columnar format called Capacitor. Every column is encoded and compressed separately, allowing queries to read only the columns they need (avoiding wasted I/O). Capacitor was introduced in 2016 as a successor to the older ColumnIO format; it supports complex nested and repeated fields using Dremel’s definition/repetition-level encoding, and it even allows BigQuery to operate directly on compressed data. This yields very high compression ratios and fast scan throughput on analytic workloads. Colossus (Distributed Storage System)  — Colossus is Google’s global distributed file system (the successor to GFS) that underpins BigQuery’s storage. Each BigQuery table is broken into many files on Colossus across Google’s data centers. Colossus handles replication and recovery so there’s no single point of failure. It provides high durability and throughput, and it lets BigQuery scale to petabytes of data effortlessly. In fact, BigQuery “leverages the ColumnIO columnar format… to store data in Colossus” so that it can scale “to dozens of Petabytes in storage seamlessly, without paying the penalty of attaching much more expensive compute resources”. In practice, Colossus lets BigQuery deliver performance akin to in-memory systems while using much cheaper, highly parallelized storage hardware. Dremel (Query Execution Engine)  — Dremel is the massive, multi-tenant query engine that runs BigQuery SQL jobs. It parses your SQL into a serving tree that has three tiers: a root node, many intermediate nodes (called mixers), and thousands of leaf nodes. The root node takes your query, plans it, and splits it into smaller tasks. Mixers sit in the middle of the tree: they optimize and fan out tasks (or aggregate partial results). The leaf nodes at the bottom (called slots) do the actual data scanning and computation. Each leaf (slot) reads its assigned columns from Colossus, applies filters and partial aggregations, and returns results upward. In this way, a single SQL query is distributed across thousands of workers in parallel. Dremel dynamically apportions slots for each query, so large queries can use thousands of slots simultaneously. Detailed Explanation covered later. Borg (Cluster Manager) — Borg is Google’s cluster management and job scheduler (the predecessor to Kubernetes) that powers BigQuery compute. Behind the scenes, BigQuery runs on Borg clusters composed of tens of thousands of machines and hundreds of thousands of CPU cores. When you run a query, Borg allocates the needed servers and CPU cores to build your Dremel execution tree. Borg also handles hardware failures: it automatically reschedules work if machines or disks fail, so queries continue without interruption. In short, Borg makes BigQuery multi-tenant, reliable, and scalable — Google can run your jobs on a fraction of its vast capacity, and failures in the datacenter are hidden from the user. Jupiter (High-Speed Network) — Jupiter is Google’s high-bandwidth datacenter network fabric. It delivers on the order of petabit-per-second bisection bandwidth (enough for ~100,000 machines at 10 Gbps each). This ultra-fast network is critical to BigQuery’s performance. It lets BigQuery move data between storage and compute (and between nodes) with minimal latency or bottlenecks. For example, when executing a query that requires shuffling data, BigQuery can push terabytes of data across Jupiter in seconds, something not possible with ordinary cloud networks. In effect, Jupiter makes data locality irrelevant — any slot can quickly pull data from any disk in the fleet. The result is a fully cloud-native warehouse: BigQuery can read huge amounts of data from Colossus directly for each query. Query Execution: The Dremel Tree BigQuery runs each SQL query through a multi-stage tree of computation nodes. The diagram below illustrates a typical Dremel execution tree: A   Dremel execution tree for a BigQuery query. The root node dispatches tasks to multiple mixers (intermediates), which fan out work to many leaf nodes (slots) that read data and compute in parallel. When you submit a query, the root server parses it and delegates work down the tree. In general: Root node: Receives the SQL, resolves metadata (table schemas), and splits the query into sub-tasks. Task distribution: The root dispatches these tasks to multiple intermediate nodes (mixers). Mixers: Each mixer may further optimize the task (e.g. prune partitions) and fan out copies to many leaf nodes. Leaf nodes (slots): Each leaf (a slot) scans its portion of the columnar data in Colossus and performs filters, aggregations or calculations. For example, one leaf might count rows in partition A while another does partition B. Aggregation: The leaves send their partial results back up to the mixers, which merge or aggregate them. Final result: The aggregated data flows back to the root node, which assembles the final result and returns it to the user. This tree-structured execution is extremely parallel — thousands of leaf slots work in parallel on different data shards. It lets BigQuery scan massive tables very quickly. Because each leaf reads only the needed columns (courtesy of columnar storage) and runs in parallel, BigQuery can process terabytes of data per second across the cluster. In effect, even very large queries become fast because they are embarrassingly parallel in Dremel’s model. BigQuery Slots: The Compute Units A slot in BigQuery is its fundamental unit of computation — essentially a virtual CPU core for query processing. In the Dremel tree, each leaf node is a slot that does the heavy work of reading and computing. BigQuery automatically decides how many slots to assign to each query based on its size and complexity. Large queries can be granted thousands of slots in parallel, while smaller queries use fewer. Think of slots like workers on an assembly line: more slots means more parallel work. If a query can be split into many pieces, giving it more slots speeds it up — each slot reads its slice of data concurrently. Conversely, if slots are scarce or queries are very small, they may run more serially and take longer. (Google also offers slot reservations and flat-rate models for special needs, but by default, it’s all managed behind the scenes.) By default, a BigQuery project can use up to 2,000 on-demand slots at once. In practice, this means even ad-hoc analysts often get hundreds of cores at query time. Since each slot operates on independent data blocks, increasing slots yields a near-linear speedup until the data is exhausted. In short, slots provide BigQuery’s parallel compute horsepower. Why Decoupled Architecture Matters B igQuery’s separated storage/compute design has huge practical benefits for engineers: Cost Efficiency: Because compute and storage are independent, you only pay for what you use. You don’t have to buy big fixed clusters up front. If your data grows, BigQuery can handle the new TB of data in Colossus without needing more compute nodes. Similarly, if a workload spikes, BigQuery can provision extra slots on the fly — without any delay in resizing storage. This makes BigQuery very cost-effective for bursty or growing workloads compared to systems where you must pre-provision resources. Elastic Scaling: As your data or query load grows, BigQuery can elastically expand. For example, BigQuery “allows users to scale to dozens of petabytes of data stored seamlessly, without paying the penalty of attaching much more expensive compute resources”. Because Google’s Borg+Jupiter can handle massive multitenancy, running more queries or bigger scans simply means using more of an already-large pool of machines. In practice, no single query can “starve” the system — as BigQuery’s own documentation notes, its vast size and multitenancy mean it “scales seamlessly with concurrency demands”. High Performance: The separation also enables extreme parallelism. BigQuery can read directly from Colossus over Jupiter, avoiding the slow data-loading step common in cloud-warehouse architectures. In fact, Google reports examples like scanning 28 GB of data in under 2 seconds by reading only the needed columns. The combination of columnar storage and thousands of parallel slots lets BigQuery process terabytes per second of data. In short, decoupling means BigQuery can treat storage as a passive “data lake” and summon compute on demand, giving data engineers fast, scale-out SQL without the pains of scaling clusters. In summary, BigQuery’s architecture — with Capacitor (columns), Colossus (distributed storage), Dremel (query engine), Borg (cluster manager), and Jupiter (network) — is engineered for massive parallelism and elastic scale. For data engineers, this means lightning-fast analytics on huge datasets with essentially zero operational overhead. You never manage the servers; instead you focus on SQL, while BigQuery handles the parallel execution and scaling behind the scenes. Performance & Scalability Highlights: By combining these components, BigQuery can achieve incredible throughput. Colossus partitions tables into shards so that hundreds or thousands of leaf slots can scan in parallel. Capacitor’s high compression and column pruning reduce I/O. Borg and Jupiter coordinate thousands of CPUs so that a complex query sweeps through petabytes of data in seconds. In one example, Google showed BigQuery scanning 28 GB and returning a result in under 2 seconds by using only 327 MB of data after column filtering. In practice, BigQuery can scan on the order of terabytes per second. All of this is transparent to the user: BigQuery truly provides “one-click” SQL at petabyte scale. Query Execution Example: A simple query like SELECT year, COUNT(*) FROM table WHERE category='X' GROUP BY year would be handled as follows: the root node parses the SQL and identifies the table schema, then splits the work by year. Mixers route these subtasks to many leaf slots. Each slot reads its assigned rows for ‘X’ from storage, counts them, and returns a partial count. The mixers then aggregate the counts by year and the root returns the final tallies. From the user’s perspective it’s one query; under the hood it was thousands of machines working together in a tree, shuffling only aggregated results back up. In conclusion, understanding BigQuery’s core components helps explain why it’s so fast, scalable, and easy to use. The serverless, decoupled design removes most operational concerns: whether you have 10 GB or 10 PB of data, BigQuery will simply expand to fit. By leveraging Google’s infrastructure (Borg for compute, Colossus for storage, Jupiter for network) and the Dremel execution model, BigQuery delivers a high-performance analytics engine that runs “invisibly” on the cloud. Working in the Field of Data Engineering, and love to explore new things
Source: 2026-05-31 High-Performance Large Language Model Serving Architectures on Google Cloud | by Don McCasland | MediumType: Pasted TextWebSync metadata title: High-Performance Large Language Model Serving Architectures on Google Cloud | by Don McCasland | Medium url: https://medium.com/@donmccasland_57353/high-performance-large-language-model-serving-architectures-on-google-cloud-7ed8b809ae26 date: 2026-05-31T01:29:01.715Z parsing method: defuddle Sitemap The rapid evolution of generative artificial intelligence has fundamentally altered the requirements for enterprise cloud infrastructure. As global cloud spending is projected to surpass the trillion-dollar milestone in early 2026, the focus of major organizations has shifted from the novelty of large language model (LLM) training to the grueling economic and technical realities of inference at scale.1 Google Cloud, positioned as a leading provider in this space, offers a multifaceted ecosystem for serving these models, ranging from high-level managed services to granular, disaggregated Kubernetes-native stacks. This analysis explores the architectural breadth of these serving options, examining the mechanisms of Vertex AI, Cloud Run, and Google Kubernetes Engine (GKE), how to choose the right accelerator, and a comparison of modern inference engines such as vLLM and the llm-d framework. Managed Foundation Models and MaaS Endpoints The Model-as-a-Service architecture is exemplified by the Gemini family of multimodal models. These models, including the Gemini 3 and 3.1 series, are accessed through managed endpoints where the complexity of the underlying infrastructure — scaling, patching, and hardware management — is entirely abstracted from the user.4 This approach utilizes a consumption-based pricing model, often billed per token, which aligns costs directly with usage and removes the financial burden of maintaining idle compute capacity. A critical advantage of this managed approach is the native integration with broader Google Cloud data services. For instance, LLMs on Vertex AI can be connected to BigQuery and Cloud Storage, allowing the models to reason over massive enterprise datasets through semantic parsing and retrieval-augmented generation (RAG).4 This integration transforms a standard language model into a specialized data agent capable of generating structured SQL queries or Python scripts from natural language prompts, thereby democratizing data access across non-technical business units.4 The Model Garden and Open Model Ecosystem For scenarios where proprietary models do not fit, the Vertex AI Model Garden provides access to over 200 best-in-class open-weight and partner models, such as Anthropic’s Claude, Meta’s Llama, and Google’s Open Model Gemma.3 The Model Garden acts as a curated library that simplifies the discovery and deployment of these assets. When a user selects an open-weight model, Vertex AI facilitates a “self-service” deployment flow that registers the model in the Vertex AI Model Registry and deploys it to a dedicated endpoint.3 This self-service route provides significantly more flexibility than MaaS. Organizations can choose specific hardware configurations, including various NVIDIA GPU types or Google’s custom TPU accelerators, and can apply tuning recipes such as Low-Rank Adaptation (LoRA) to specialize the model on proprietary domain data.3 This flexibility is particularly relevant in 2026, as companies increasingly move away from monolithic “frontier” models in favor of smaller, distilled reasoning models that offer high performance at a fraction of the cost.10 Endpoint Architectures and Networking Isolation The technical robustness of a Vertex AI deployment is largely determined by the choice of endpoint type. The architecture of these endpoints has been refined to address the unique challenges of generative AI, such as large payload sizes and long inference durations. Dedicated endpoints are recommended for production environments because they provide resource isolation, mitigating the “noisy neighbor” effect common in multi-tenant systems.14 Furthermore, the introduction of Private Service Connect (PSC) for dedicated endpoints allows traffic to stay entirely within Google Cloud’s global backbone, reducing latency variability and enhancing the security posture for sensitive health or financial data.11 Serverless Inference via Cloud Run Cloud Run has emerged as a compelling middle ground between the full abstraction of Vertex AI and the operational intensity of Kubernetes. By introducing support for GPU-accelerated instances, Cloud Run enables serverless LLM serving that can scale to zero when inactive, significantly optimizing costs for intermittent workloads.15 GPU Hardware and Startup Performance Cloud Run currently supports NVIDIA L4 and the high-performance NVIDIA RTX PRO 6000 Blackwell GPUs.15 A defining feature of Cloud Run’s AI capability is its startup speed; instances with pre-installed drivers can start in approximately 5 seconds, allowing for rapid response to incoming traffic spikes.15 This is a drastic improvement over traditional VM-based deployments, which often require minutes to provision and initialize hardware. The resource allocation for these instances is strictly defined to ensure stable inference. For an L4 GPU, a minimum of 4 CPUs and 16 GiB of memory is required, though 8 CPUs and 32 GiB are recommended for most production LLMs.15 For the more demanding Blackwell GPUs, the minimum requirements jump to 20 CPUs and 80 GiB of memory.15 Resilience and Cost Governance To enhance availability, Cloud Run offers “zonal redundancy,” which reserves GPU capacity across multiple zones. This ensures that if a specific data center experiences an outage, traffic can be rerouted to a zone with available hardware.15 While this increases the cost per GPU-second, it provides the reliability required for mission-critical conversational assistants. However, organizations must implement careful cost governance. While Cloud Run allows setting maximum instance limits, it lacks hard dollar-based billing caps, which can lead to unexpected expenses if traffic management is not properly configured.17 Despite this, the ability to pay only for the seconds that a GPU is active remains one of the most cost-effective ways to serve low-to-medium volume LLM applications.18 Google Kubernetes Engine: The AI Hypercomputer For massive-scale deployments and high-throughput batch processing, Google Kubernetes Engine (GKE) serves as the primary orchestration layer. GKE transforms standard compute clusters into what Google terms an “AI Hypercomputer,” integrating specialized silicon, high-speed networking, and open-source software.5 GKE Inference Quickstart and AI Profiles To reduce the complexity of Kubernetes manifests, GKE provides an “Inference Quickstart” feature. This tool allows developers to use the gcloud container ai profiles list command to view pre-benchmarked configurations for specific use cases.23 These profiles suggest the optimal combination of machine type, accelerator, and serving stack (e.g., vLLM or llm-d) based on historical performance data, potentially saving months of manual tuning. For distributed models that span multiple hosts, GKE utilizes the LeaderWorkerSet (LWS) API. This API simplifies the management of groups of pods that must be co-scheduled and treated as a single logical unit, which is a requirement for multi-host TPU inference or very large GPU clusters.22 Infrastructure Choices If you’ve chosen to architect your own distributed serving platform using GKE, there are a lot of choices to make including accelerators, networks, storage, and serving frameworks. Accelerator Compute Google Cloud offers a comprehensive portfolio of high-performance compute options across custom-designed TPUs and NVIDIA GPUs. Consumption models include on-demand, long-term reservations (CUDs), cost-effective Spot VMs and the Dynamic Workload Scheduler (DWS). NVIDIA GPU Offerings: Google Cloud provides a full range of NVIDIA GPUs optimized for different workloads and VM types. These include: Blackwell Generation (Next Gen): A4 VMs (NVIDIA B200) and A4X VMs (NVIDIA GB200 NVL72). Hopper Generation: A3 VMs (NVIDIA H100) and A3 Ultra VMs (NVIDIA H200). Inference & Visualization: G2 VMs (NVIDIA L4) and G4 VMs (NVIDIA RTX 6000 Pro/Blackwell Server GPU). Legacy/Prior Generations: A2 VMs (NVIDIA A100), V100, P100, T4, P4, and K80. Google TPU Offerings: TPUs (Tensor Processing Units) are Google’s custom-designed systolic array architecture, optimized for matrix processing and high performance-per-watt efficiency. The available generations include: Next Gen: The 7th Gen TPU (Ironwood) is now GA’d Current: TPU v6e (Trillium), which offers top performance and efficiency, TPU v5e (optimized for cost-efficient inference and training), and TPU v5p (aimed at training performance) **Prior Generations:TPU v4, TPU v3 and TPU v2.Intra-Accelerator NetworkingTo address the communication requirements of distributed models, TPUs utilize Inter-Chip Interconnect (ICI), a proprietary high-speed networking technology that enables seamless memory pooling across thousands of chips.Similarly, GPU clusters leverage NVLink for high-bandwidth communication between chips within a host.AI-Optimized StorageTo eliminate I/O bottlenecks during inference, Anywhere Cache provides an SSD-backed zonal read cache for Google Cloud Storage (GCS), reducing latency by up to 96% and providing up to 2.5 TB/s of bandwidth. For workloads requiring the lowest possible latency or those already utilizing parallel file systems for training, Managed Lustre provides sub-millisecond access and up to 1 TB/s throughput.Secondary Serving and Integrated PathsBeyond the primary compute platforms, Google Cloud offers specialized paths for LLM interaction that target specific user personas and technical constraints.SQL-Native Inference in BigQueryBigQuery has evolved from a data warehouse into a managed inference engine. By supporting SQL-native inference for open models, BigQuery allows users to execute model predictions using standard SQL queries. This is particularly powerful for bulk processing tasks, such as sentiment analysis or entity extraction on millions of rows of data, without the need to export data to an external ML service.4High-Performance Computing and Slurm ClustersFor research teams and organizations coming from traditional high-performance computing (HPC) backgrounds, Google Cloud supports building Slurm clusters using the Cluster Director. Slurm is a highly scalable cluster management and job scheduling system, which is valued in the HPC community for its ability to intelligently allocate resources, support massively parallel computing across thousands of compute nodes, and maximize throughput through sophisticated job queuing and fair-share policies. While this requires the most DevOps expertise, it offers the highest degree of control over the hardware and interconnect stack.25Serving FrameworksWhen building a distributed inference serving cluster, a key choice is which serving framework to use. While some models will lock you into specific serving choices, the vast majority of models available today can be served by SGLang, TRT-LLM, vLLM, and llm-d. vLLM and llm-d are the most commonly used across Google Cloud.vLLM: The High-Throughput Inference EngineThe vLLM framework has become the de facto standard for efficient LLM serving in 2026. Its primary objective is to maximize the utilization of expensive GPU and TPU resources by addressing the memory bottlenecks inherent in the Transformer architecture.5PagedAttention and Memory EfficiencyThe core innovation of vLLM is PagedAttention, which draws inspiration from virtual memory paging in operating systems. Traditional model servers allocate a contiguous block of memory for the KV cache of each request. Because the final length of a response is unknown at the start, this leads to significant internal fragmentation and “overserving” of memory. PagedAttention partitions the KV cache into small, non-contiguous blocks, allowing the model to utilize almost 100% of available VRAM.20This efficiency enables vLLM to handle up to 4 times more concurrent sequences than standard implementations on the same hardware.20 For a production API service, this translates directly to a reduction in the number of required GPU nodes, often cutting monthly infrastructure costs by up to 75%.20Continuous Batching and Unified TPU SupportUnlike static batching, which waits for all requests in a batch to complete before starting a new one, vLLM utilizes “continuous” or “rolling” batching. As soon as a single request finishes generating its final token, a new request from the queue can enter the batch. This ensures that the GPU never sits idle while waiting for the longest-running sequence to finish, resulting in throughput gains of 14–24x over naive implementations.20In late 2025, vLLM announced a unified backend for Cloud TPUs. This allows PyTorch model definitions to run performantly on TPUs without code changes, leveraging JAX’s mature primitives under the hood to generate high-performance HLO graphs for the XLA compiler.27 This unification provides developers with the flexibility to choose the best hardware for their cost-performance ratio without being locked into a specific framework.llm-d: The Disaggregated Distributed StackWhile vLLM serves as the execution engine, llm-d is a Kubernetes-native serving stack designed to provide “well-lit paths” for at-scale inference.28 Think of llm-d as ‘distributed vLLM.’ Developed as an open-source collaboration between Google, Red Hat, IBM, and NVIDIA, llm-d focuses on the orchestration challenges of the leading edge.28Disaggregated Prefill and DecodeOne of the most significant architectural shifts introduced by llm-d is the disaggregation of the inference pipeline. LLM inference consists of two distinct phases:Prefill: Processes the entire input prompt in parallel to build the initial KV cache. This is compute-bound and benefits from high-performance cores.19Decode: Generates tokens one by one based on the previous context. This is memory-bandwidth bound and often bottlenecked by the speed at which weights can be loaded into the GPU.11By splitting these phases into independent instances, llm-d allows organizations to scale their infrastructure according to the specific bottleneck of their workload. Early benchmarks show that this disaggregation can lead to a 2x to 3x improvement in Time-to-First-Token (TTFT), creating much more responsive applications for use cases like code completion or real-time translation.26Intelligent Scheduling and Tiered KV Cachingllm-d replaces simple round-robin load balancing with a “vLLM-aware” inference scheduler. This scheduler, integrated with the GKE Inference Gateway and Envoy proxy, routes requests to specific nodes based on predicted prefix-cache hits.26 If a new request shares a long system prompt with a previous request, the scheduler will send it to the node where that prompt’s KV cache is already stored, drastically reducing the compute required for the prefill phase.28Furthermore, llm-d introduces a multi-tier KV cache hierarchy. This system can offload intermediate values from expensive GPU HBM to cheaper host RAM or local SSDs (e.g., using LMCache), significantly expanding the context length and concurrency that a single cluster can handle.Comparative Analysis: vLLM vs. llm-dWhile llm-d utilizes vLLM as its default model server, the two should be understood as complementary layers of the serving stack rather than direct competitors.Architectural Differences and Hardware SupportThe fundamental difference lies in the scope of management. vLLM is a library and engine focused on the efficiency of a single model instance. In contrast, llm-d is a complete orchestration framework that manages the lifecycle, routing, and scaling of a distributed fleet of vLLM instances.23Performance ImplicationsIn high-concurrency scenarios (e.g., 1,200 concurrent threads), the prefix-aware routing of llm-d (often using Consistent Hashing with Bounded Loads) has been shown to cut TTFT by 95% and boost throughput by 127% compared to naive vLLM deployments on GKE.26 Standalone vLLM remains the superior choice for simpler, single-host interactive use or for developers who want a lightweight Python-first experience.20 However, for “billion-user scale” production environments, the disaggregated and distributed nature of llm-d is required to meet modern latency Service Level Objectives (SLOs).28Conclusion: Strategic Recommendations for 2026The selection of an LLM serving strategy on Google Cloud is no longer a choice between “easy” and “hard,” but rather an architectural decision based on the specific traffic patterns and latency requirements of the application.The Indian Economic Survey 2025–26 serves as a pertinent reminder that the pursuit of frontier models must be balanced against the constraints of energy and infrastructure costs.**10 For most organizations, this means prioritizing the “middle-out” approach: starting with managed Vertex AI endpoints for rapid iteration, then migrating to GKE with vLLM for sustained, high-volume traffic to capture significant cost savings.7 The emergence of llm-d represents the next frontier in this journey. As models grow larger and agentic workflows require longer context windows, the ability to disaggregate the inference pipeline and utilize tiered KV caching will transition from a “nice-to-have” to a necessity.28 By leveraging the GKE Inference Gateway and the specialized networking of the AI Hypercomputer, developers can ensure that their AI services remain responsive and economically viable in a competitive global market.5 Ultimately, the goal of LLM serving in 2026 is to reduce the “time to state-of-the-art performance” while maintaining the highest standards of data sovereignty and cost-efficiency.29 Whether via the managed abstraction of Vertex AI or the distributed serving stack of llm-d, Google Cloud’s platform offers the necessary infrastructure to handle the growing demands
